I never may believe\nThese antique fables nor these fairy toys.\nLovers and madmen have such seething brains,\nSuch shaping fantasies, that apprehend\nMore than cool reason ever comprehends.\nThe lunatic, the lover, and the poet\nAre of imagination all compact.\nOne sees more devils than vast hell can hold:\nThat is the madman. The lover, all as frantic,\nSees Helens beauty in a brow of Egypt.\nThe poets eye, in a fine frenzy rolling,\nDoth glance from heaven to Earth, from Earth to\nheaven,\nAnd as imagination bodies forth\nThe forms of things unknown, the poets pen\nTurns them to shapes and gives to airy nothing\nA local habitation and a name.\nSuch tricks hath strong imagination\nThat, if it would but apprehend some joy,\nIt comprehends some bringer of that joy.\nOr in the night, imagining some fear,\nHow easy is a bush supposed a bear!\nHIPPOLYTA\nBut all the story of the night told over,\nAnd all their minds transfigured so together,\nMore witnesseth than fancys images\nAnd grows to something of great constancy,\nBut, howsoever, strange and admirable.\n\nEnter Lovers: Lysander, Demetrius, Hermia, and Helena.\n\nTHESEUS\nHere come the lovers full of joy and mirth.\nJoy, gentle friends! Joy and fresh days of love\nAccompany your hearts!\nLYSANDER  More than to us\nWait in your royal walks, your board, your bed!\nTHESEUS\nCome now, what masques, what dances shall we\nhave\nTo wear away this long age of three hours\nBetween our after-supper and bedtime?\nWhere is our usual manager of mirth?\nWhat revels are in hand? Is there no play\nTo ease the anguish of a torturing hour?\nCall Philostrate.\nPHILOSTRATE, coming forward  Here, mighty Theseus.\nTHESEUS\nSay what abridgment have you for this evening,\nWhat masque, what music? How shall we beguile\nThe lazy time if not with some delight?\nPHILOSTRATE, giving Theseus a paper\nThere is a brief how many sports are ripe.\nMake choice of which your Highness will see first.\nTHESEUS\nThe battle with the Centaurs, to be sung\nBy an Athenian eunuch to the harp.\nWell none of that. That have I told my love\nIn glory of my kinsman Hercules.\nThe riot of the tipsy Bacchanals,\nTearing the Thracian singer in their rage.\nThat is an old device, and it was played\nWhen I from Thebes came last a conqueror.\nThe thrice-three Muses mourning for the death\nOf learning, late deceased in beggary.\nThat is some satire, keen and critical,\nNot sorting with a nuptial ceremony.\nA tedious brief scene of young Pyramus\nAnd his love Thisbe, very tragical mirth.\nMerry and tragical? Tedious and brief?\nThat is hot ice and wondrous strange snow!\nHow shall we find the concord of this discord?\nPHILOSTRATE\nA play there is, my lord, some ten words long\n(Which is as brief as I have known a play),\nBut by ten words, my lord, it is too long,\nWhich makes it tedious; for in all the play,\nThere is not one word apt, one player fitted.\nAnd tragical, my noble lord, it is.\nFor Pyramus therein doth kill himself,\nWhich, when I saw rehearsed, I must confess,\nMade mine eyes water; but more merry tears\nThe passion of loud laughter never shed.\nTHESEUS\nWhat are they that do play it?\nPHILOSTRATE\nHard-handed men that work in Athens here,\nWhich never labored in their minds till now,\nAnd now have toiled their unbreathed memories\nWith this same play, against your nuptial.\nTHESEUS\nAnd we will hear it.\nPHILOSTRATE  No, my noble lord,\nIt is not for you. I have heard it over,\nAnd it is nothing, nothing in the world,\nUnless you can find sport in their intents,\nExtremely stretched and conned with cruel pain\nTo do you service.\nTHESEUS  I will hear that play,\nFor never anything can be amiss\nWhen simpleness and duty tender it.\nGo, bring them inand take your places, ladies.\nPhilostrate exits.\nHIPPOLYTA\nI love not to see wretchedness oercharged,\nAnd duty in his service perishing.\nTHESEUS\nWhy, gentle sweet, you shall see no such thing.\nHIPPOLYTA\nHe says they can do nothing in this kind.\nTHESEUS\nThe kinder we, to give them thanks for nothing.\nOur sport shall be to take what they mistake;\nAnd what poor duty cannot do, noble respect\nTakes it in might, not merit.\nWhere I have come, great clerks have purposd\nTo greet me with premeditated welcomes,\nWhere I have seen them shiver and look pale,\nMake periods in the midst of sentences,\nThrottle their practiced accent in their fears,\nAnd in conclusion dumbly have broke off,\nNot paying me a welcome. Trust me, sweet,\nOut of this silence yet I picked a welcome,\nAnd in the modesty of fearful duty,\nI read as much as from the rattling tongue\nOf saucy and audacious eloquence.\nLove, therefore, and tongue-tied simplicity\nIn least speak most, to my capacity.\n\nEnter Philostrate.\n\nPHILOSTRATE\nSo please your Grace, the Prologue is addressed.\nTHESEUS  Let him approach.\n\nEnter the Prologue.\n\nPROLOGUE\nIf we offend, it is with our goodwill.\n   That you should think we come not to offend,\nBut with goodwill. To show our simple skill,\n   That is the true beginning of our end.\nConsider, then, we come but in despite.\n   We do not come, as minding to content you,\nOur true intent is. All for your delight\n   We are not here. That you should here repent\n   you,\nThe actors are at hand, and, by their show,\nYou shall know all that you are like to know.\nPrologue exits.\nTHESEUS  This fellow doth not stand upon points.\nLYSANDER  He hath rid his prologue like a rough colt;\nhe knows not the stop. A good moral, my lord: it is\nnot enough to speak, but to speak true.\nHIPPOLYTA  Indeed he hath played on this prologue like\na child on a recordera sound, but not in\ngovernment.\nTHESEUS  His speech was like a tangled chainnothing\nimpaired, but all disordered. Who is next?\n\nEnter Pyramus (Bottom), and Thisbe (Flute), and\x0bWall (Snout), and Moonshine (Starveling), and Lion\x0b(Snug), and Prologue (Quince).\n\nQUINCE, as Prologue\nGentles, perchance you wonder at this show.\n   But wonder on, till truth make all things plain.\nThis man is Pyramus, if you would know.\n   This beauteous lady Thisbe is certain.\nThis man with lime and roughcast doth present\n   Wall, that vile wall which did these lovers\n   sunder;\nAnd through Walls chink, poor souls, they are\ncontent\n   To whisper, at the which let no man wonder.\nThis man, with lantern, dog, and bush of thorn,\n   Presenteth Moonshine, for, if you will know,\nBy moonshine did these lovers think no scorn\n   To meet at Ninus tomb, there, there to woo.\nThis grisly beast (which Lion hight by name)\n   The trusty Thisbe coming first by night\nDid scare away or rather did affright;\nAnd, as she fled, her mantle she did fall,\n   Which Lion vile with bloody mouth did stain.\nAnon comes Pyramus, sweet youth and tall,\n   And finds his trusty Thisbes mantle slain.\nWhereat, with blade, with bloody blameful blade,\n   He bravely broached his boiling bloody breast.\nAnd Thisbe, tarrying in mulberry shade,\n   His dagger drew, and died. For all the rest,\nLet Lion, Moonshine, Wall, and lovers twain\nAt large discourse, while here they do remain.\nTHESEUS  I wonder if the lion be to speak.\nDEMETRIUS  No wonder, my lord. One lion may when\nmany asses do.\nLion, Thisbe, Moonshine, and Prologue exit.\nSNOUT, as Wall\nIn this same interlude it doth befall\nThat I, one Snout by name, present a wall;\nAnd such a wall as I would have you think\nThat had in it a crannied hole or chink,\nThrough which the lovers, Pyramus and Thisbe,\nDid whisper often, very secretly.\nThis loam, this roughcast, and this stone doth show\nThat I am that same wall. The truth is so.\nAnd this the cranny is, right and sinister,\nThrough which the fearful lovers are to whisper.\nTHESEUS  Would you desire lime and hair to speak\nbetter?\nDEMETRIUS  It is the wittiest partition that ever I heard\ndiscourse, my lord.\nTHESEUS  Pyramus draws near the wall. Silence.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nO grim-looked night! O night with hue so black!\n   O night, which ever art when day is not!\nO night! O night! Alack, alack, alack!\n   I fear my Thisbes promise is forgot.\nAnd thou, O wall, O sweet, O lovely wall,\n   That standst between her fathers ground and\n   mine,\nThou wall, O wall, O sweet and lovely wall,\n   Show me thy chink to blink through with mine\n   eyne.\nThanks, courteous wall. Jove shield thee well for\nthis.\n   But what see I? No Thisbe do I see.\nO wicked wall, through whom I see no bliss,\n   Cursed be thy stones for thus deceiving me!\nTHESEUS  The wall, methinks, being sensible, should\ncurse again.\nBOTTOM  No, in truth, sir, he should not. Deceiving\nme is Thisbes cue. She is to enter now, and I am\nto spy her through the wall. You shall see it will fall\npat as I told you. Yonder she comes.\n\nEnter Thisbe (Flute).\n\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nO wall, full often hast thou heard my moans\n   For parting my fair Pyramus and me.\nMy cherry lips have often kissed thy stones,\n   Thy stones with lime and hair knit up in thee.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nI see a voice! Now will I to the chink\n   To spy an I can hear my Thisbes face.\nThisbe?\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nMy love! Thou art my love, I think.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\n   Think what thou wilt, I am thy lovers grace,\nAnd, like Limander, am I trusty still.\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nAnd I like Helen, till the Fates me kill.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nNot Shafalus to Procrus was so true.\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nAs Shafalus to Procrus, I to you.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nO kiss me through the hole of this vile wall.\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nI kiss the walls hole, not your lips at all.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nWilt thou at Ninnys tomb meet me straightway?\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nTide life, tide death, I come without delay.\nBottom and Flute exit.\nSNOUT, as Wall\nThus have I, Wall, my part dischargd so,\nAnd, being done, thus Wall away doth go.\tHe exits.\nTHESEUS  Now is the wall down between the two\nneighbors.\nDEMETRIUS  No remedy, my lord, when walls are so\nwillful to hear without warning.\nHIPPOLYTA  This is the silliest stuff that ever I heard.\nTHESEUS  The best in this kind are but shadows, and\nthe worst are no worse, if imagination amend\nthem.\nHIPPOLYTA  It must be your imagination, then, and not\ntheirs.\nTHESEUS  If we imagine no worse of them than they of\nthemselves, they may pass for excellent men. Here\ncome two noble beasts in, a man and a lion.\n\nEnter Lion (Snug) and Moonshine (Starveling).\n\nSNUG, as Lion\nYou ladies, you whose gentle hearts do fear\n   The smallest monstrous mouse that creeps on\n   floor,\nMay now perchance both quake and tremble here,\n   When lion rough in wildest rage doth roar.\nThen know that I, as Snug the joiner, am\nA lion fell, nor else no lions dam;\nFor if I should as lion come in strife\nInto this place, twere pity on my life.\nTHESEUS  A very gentle beast, and of a good\nconscience.\nDEMETRIUS  The very best at a beast, my lord, that eer I\nsaw.\nLYSANDER  This lion is a very fox for his valor.\nTHESEUS  True, and a goose for his discretion.\nDEMETRIUS  Not so, my lord, for his valor cannot carry\nhis discretion, and the fox carries the goose.\nTHESEUS  His discretion, I am sure, cannot carry his\nvalor, for the goose carries not the fox. It is well.\nLeave it to his discretion, and let us listen to the\nMoon.\nSTARVELING, as Moonshine\nThis lanthorn doth the hornd moon present.\nDEMETRIUS  He should have worn the horns on his\nhead.\nTHESEUS  He is no crescent, and his horns are invisible\nwithin the circumference.\nSTARVELING, as Moonshine\nThis lanthorn doth the hornd moon present.\nMyself the man i th moon do seem to be.\nTHESEUS  This is the greatest error of all the rest; the\nman should be put into the lanthorn. How is it else\nthe man i th moon?\nDEMETRIUS  He dares not come there for the candle,\nfor you see, it is already in snuff.\nHIPPOLYTA  I am aweary of this moon. Would he would\nchange.\nTHESEUS  It appears by his small light of discretion that\nhe is in the wane; but yet, in courtesy, in all reason,\nwe must stay the time.\nLYSANDER  Proceed, Moon.\nSTARVELING, as Moonshine   All that I have to say is to tell\nyou that the lanthorn is the moon, I the man i th\nmoon, this thornbush my thornbush, and this dog\nmy dog.\nDEMETRIUS  Why, all these should be in the lanthorn,\nfor all these are in the moon. But silence. Here\ncomes Thisbe.\n\nEnter Thisbe (Flute).\n\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\nThis is old Ninnys tomb. Where is my love?\nSNUG, as Lion  O!\nThe Lion roars. Thisbe runs off,\x0bdropping her mantle.\nDEMETRIUS  Well roared, Lion.\nTHESEUS  Well run, Thisbe.\nHIPPOLYTA  Well shone, Moon. Truly, the Moon shines\nwith a good grace.\nLion worries the mantle.\nTHESEUS  Well moused, Lion.\n\nEnter Pyramus (Bottom).\n\nDEMETRIUS  And then came Pyramus.\nLion exits.\nLYSANDER  And so the lion vanished.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nSweet Moon, I thank thee for thy sunny beams.\n   I thank thee, Moon, for shining now so bright,\nFor by thy gracious, golden, glittering gleams,\n   I trust to take of truest Thisbe sight.\n      But stay! O spite!\n      But mark, poor knight,\n   What dreadful dole is here!\n      Eyes, do you see!\n      How can it be!\n   O dainty duck! O dear!\n      Thy mantle good\n      What, stained with blood?\n   Approach, ye Furies fell!\n      O Fates, come, come,\n      Cut thread and thrum,\n   Quail, crush, conclude, and quell!\nTHESEUS  This passion, and the death of a dear friend,\nwould go near to make a man look sad.\nHIPPOLYTA  Beshrew my heart but I pity the man.\nBOTTOM, as Pyramus\nO, wherefore, Nature, didst thou lions frame,\n   Since lion vile hath here deflowered my dear,\nWhich isno, nowhich was the fairest dame\n   That lived, that loved, that liked, that looked with\n   cheer?\n      Come, tears, confound!\n      Out, sword, and wound\n   The pap of Pyramus;\n      Ay, that left pap,\n      Where heart doth hop.\tPyramus stabs himself.\n   Thus die I, thus, thus, thus.\n      Now am I dead;\n      Now am I fled;\n   My soul is in the sky.\n      Tongue, lose thy light!\n      Moon, take thy flight!\tMoonshine exits.\n   Now die, die, die, die, die.\tPyramus falls.\nDEMETRIUS  No die, but an ace for him, for he is but\none.\nLYSANDER  Less than an ace, man, for he is dead, he is\nnothing.\nTHESEUS  With the help of a surgeon he might yet\nrecover and yet prove an ass.\nHIPPOLYTA  How chance Moonshine is gone before\nThisbe comes back and finds her lover?\nTHESEUS  She will find him by starlight.\n\nEnter Thisbe (Flute).\n\nHere she comes, and her passion ends the play.\nHIPPOLYTA  Methinks she should not use a long one for\nsuch a Pyramus. I hope she will be brief.\nDEMETRIUS  A mote will turn the balance, which Pyramus,\nwhich Thisbe, is the better: he for a man, God\nwarrant us; she for a woman, God bless us.\nLYSANDER  She hath spied him already with those\nsweet eyes.\nDEMETRIUS  And thus she means, videlicet\nFLUTE, as Thisbe\n\t      Asleep, my love?\n\t      What, dead, my dove?\n\t   O Pyramus, arise!\n\t      Speak, speak. Quite dumb?\n\t      Dead? Dead? A tomb\n\t   Must cover thy sweet eyes.\n\t      These lily lips,\n\t      This cherry nose,\n\t   These yellow cowslip cheeks\n\t      Are gone, are gone!\n\t      Lovers, make moan;\n\t   His eyes were green as leeks.\n\t      O Sisters Three,\n\t      Come, come to me\n\t   With hands as pale as milk.\n\t      Lay them in gore,\n\t      Since you have shore\n\t   With shears his thread of silk.\n\t      Tongue, not a word!\n\t      Come, trusty sword,\n\t   Come, blade, my breast imbrue!\nThisbe stabs herself.\n\t      And farewell, friends.\n\t      Thus Thisbe ends.\n\t   Adieu, adieu, adieu.\tThisbe falls.\nTHESEUS  Moonshine and Lion are left to bury the\ndead.\nDEMETRIUS  Ay, and Wall too.\nBottom and Flute arise.\nBOTTOM  No, I assure you, the wall is down that\nparted their fathers. Will it please you to see the\nEpilogue or to hear a Bergomask dance between\ntwo of our company?\nTHESEUS  No epilogue, I pray you. For your play needs\nno excuse. Never excuse. For when the players are\nall dead, there need none to be blamed. Marry, if\nhe that writ it had played Pyramus and hanged\nhimself in Thisbes garter, it would have been a fine\ntragedy; and so it is, truly, and very notably discharged.\nBut, come, your Bergomask. Let your\nepilogue alone.\nDance, and the players exit.\nThe iron tongue of midnight hath told twelve.\nLovers, to bed! Tis almost fairy time.\nI fear we shall outsleep the coming morn\nAs much as we this night have overwatched.\nThis palpable-gross play hath well beguiled\nThe heavy gait of night. Sweet friends, to bed.\nA fortnight hold we this solemnity\nIn nightly revels and new jollity.\tThey exit.\n\nEnter Robin Goodfellow.\n\nROBIN\n\tNow the hungry lion roars,\n\t   And the wolf behowls the moon,\n\tWhilst the heavy plowman snores,\n\t   All with weary task fordone.\n\tNow the wasted brands do glow,\n\t   Whilst the screech-owl, screeching loud,\n\tPuts the wretch that lies in woe\n\t   In remembrance of a shroud.\n\tNow it is the time of night\n\t   That the graves, all gaping wide,\n\tEvery one lets forth his sprite\n\t   In the church-way paths to glide.\n\tAnd we fairies, that do run\n\t   By the triple Hecates team\n\tFrom the presence of the sun,\n\t   Following darkness like a dream,\n\tNow are frolic. Not a mouse\n\tShall disturb this hallowed house.\n\tI am sent with broom before,\n\tTo sweep the dust behind the door.\n\nEnter Oberon and Titania, King and Queen of Fairies,\x0bwith all their train.\n\nOBERON\n\tThrough the house give glimmering light,\n\t   By the dead and drowsy fire.\n\tEvery elf and fairy sprite,\n\t   Hop as light as bird from brier,\n\tAnd this ditty after me,\n\tSing and dance it trippingly.\nTITANIA\n\tFirst rehearse your song by rote,\n\tTo each word a warbling note.\n\tHand in hand, with fairy grace,\n\tWill we sing and bless this place.\nOberon leads the Fairies in song and dance.\nOBERON\n\tNow, until the break of day,\n\tThrough this house each fairy stray.\n\tTo the best bride-bed will we,\n\tWhich by us shall blessd be,\n\tAnd the issue there create\n\tEver shall be fortunate.\n\tSo shall all the couples three\n\tEver true in loving be,\n\tAnd the blots of Natures hand\n\tShall not in their issue stand.\n\tNever mole, harelip, nor scar,\n\tNor mark prodigious, such as are\n\tDespisd in nativity,\n\tShall upon their children be.\n\tWith this field-dew consecrate\n\tEvery fairy take his gait,\n\tAnd each several chamber bless,\n\tThrough this palace, with sweet peace.\n\tAnd the owner of it blest,\n\tEver shall in safety rest.\n\tTrip away. Make no stay.\n\tMeet me all by break of day.\nAll but Robin exit.\nROBIN\n\tIf we shadows have offended,\n\tThink but this and all is mended:\n\tThat you have but slumbered here\n\tWhile these visions did appear.\n\tAnd this weak and idle theme,\n\tNo more yielding but a dream,\n\tGentles, do not reprehend.\n\tIf you pardon, we will mend.\n\tAnd, as I am an honest Puck,\n\tIf we have unearnd luck\n\tNow to scape the serpents tongue,\n\tWe will make amends ere long.\n\tElse the Puck a liar call.\n\tSo good night unto you all.\n\tGive me your hands, if we be friends,\n\tAnd Robin shall restore amends.\nHe 'Enter Theseus, Hippolyta, and Philostrate, with others.\n\nTHESEUS\nNow, fair Hippolyta, our nuptial hour\nDraws on apace. Four happy days bring in\nAnother moon. But, O, methinks how slow\nThis old moon wanes! She lingers my desires\nLike to a stepdame or a dowager\nLong withering out a young mans revenue.\nHIPPOLYTA\nFour days will quickly steep themselves in night;\nFour nights will quickly dream away the time;\nAnd then the moon, like to a silver bow\nNew-bent in heaven, shall behold the night\nOf our solemnities.\nTHESEUS  Go, Philostrate,\nStir up the Athenian youth to merriments.\nAwake the pert and nimble spirit of mirth.\nTurn melancholy forth to funerals;\nThe pale companion is not for our pomp.\nPhilostrate exits.\nHippolyta, I wooed thee with my sword\nAnd won thy love doing thee injuries,\nBut I will wed thee in another key,\nWith pomp, with triumph, and with reveling.\n\nEnter Egeus and his daughter Hermia, and Lysander \x0b and Demetrius.\n\nEGEUS\nHappy be Theseus, our renownd duke!\nTHESEUS\nThanks, good Egeus. Whats the news with thee?\nEGEUS\nFull of vexation come I, with complaint\nAgainst my child, my daughter Hermia.\nStand forth, Demetrius.My noble lord,\nThis man hath my consent to marry her.\nStand forth, Lysander.And, my gracious duke,\nThis man hath bewitched the bosom of my child.\nThou, thou, Lysander, thou hast given her rhymes\nAnd interchanged love tokens with my child.\nThou hast by moonlight at her window sung\nWith feigning voice verses of feigning love\nAnd stoln the impression of her fantasy\nWith bracelets of thy hair, rings, gauds, conceits,\nKnacks, trifles, nosegays, sweetmeatsmessengers\nOf strong prevailment in unhardened youth.\nWith cunning hast thou filched my daughters heart,\nTurned her obedience (which is due to me)\nTo stubborn harshness.And, my gracious duke,\nBe it so she will not here before your Grace\nConsent to marry with Demetrius,\nI beg the ancient privilege of Athens:\nAs she is mine, I may dispose of her,\nWhich shall be either to this gentleman\nOr to her death, according to our law\nImmediately provided in that case.\nTHESEUS\nWhat say you, Hermia? Be advised, fair maid.\nTo you, your father should be as a god,\nOne that composed your beauties, yea, and one\nTo whom you are but as a form in wax\nBy him imprinted, and within his power\nTo leave the figure or disfigure it.\nDemetrius is a worthy gentleman.\nHERMIA\nSo is Lysander.\nTHESEUS  In himself he is,\nBut in this kind, wanting your fathers voice,\nThe other must be held the worthier.\nHERMIA\nI would my father looked but with my eyes.\nTHESEUS\nRather your eyes must with his judgment look.\nHERMIA\nI do entreat your Grace to pardon me.\nI know not by what power I am made bold,\nNor how it may concern my modesty\nIn such a presence here to plead my thoughts;\nBut I beseech your Grace that I may know\nThe worst that may befall me in this case\nIf I refuse to wed Demetrius.\nTHESEUS\nEither to die the death or to abjure\nForever the society of men.\nTherefore, fair Hermia, question your desires,\nKnow of your youth, examine well your blood,\nWhether (if you yield not to your fathers choice)\nYou can endure the livery of a nun,\nFor aye to be in shady cloister mewed,\nTo live a barren sister all your life,\nChanting faint hymns to the cold fruitless moon.\nThrice-blessd they that master so their blood\nTo undergo such maiden pilgrimage,\nBut earthlier happy is the rose distilled\nThan that which, withering on the virgin thorn,\nGrows, lives, and dies in single blessedness.\nHERMIA\nSo will I grow, so live, so die, my lord,\nEre I will yield my virgin patent up\nUnto his Lordship whose unwishd yoke\nMy soul consents not to give sovereignty.\nTHESEUS\nTake time to pause, and by the next new moon\n(The sealing day betwixt my love and me\nFor everlasting bond of fellowship),\nUpon that day either prepare to die\nFor disobedience to your fathers will,\nOr else to wed Demetrius, as he would,\nOr on Dianas altar to protest\nFor aye austerity and single life.\nDEMETRIUS\nRelent, sweet Hermia, and, Lysander, yield\nThy crazd title to my certain right.\nLYSANDER\nYou have her fathers love, Demetrius.\nLet me have Hermias. Do you marry him.\nEGEUS\nScornful Lysander, true, he hath my love;\nAnd what is mine my love shall render him.\nAnd she is mine, and all my right of her\nI do estate unto Demetrius.\nLYSANDER, to Theseus\nI am, my lord, as well derived as he,\nAs well possessed. My love is more than his;\nMy fortunes every way as fairly ranked\n(If not with vantage) as Demetrius;\nAnd (which is more than all these boasts can be)\nI am beloved of beauteous Hermia.\nWhy should not I then prosecute my right?\nDemetrius, Ill avouch it to his head,\nMade love to Nedars daughter, Helena,\nAnd won her soul; and she, sweet lady, dotes,\nDevoutly dotes, dotes in idolatry,\nUpon this spotted and inconstant man.\nTHESEUS\nI must confess that I have heard so much,\nAnd with Demetrius thought to have spoke thereof;\nBut, being overfull of self-affairs,\nMy mind did lose it.But, Demetrius, come,\nAnd come, Egeus; you shall go with me.\nI have some private schooling for you both.\nFor you, fair Hermia, look you arm yourself\nTo fit your fancies to your fathers will,\nOr else the law of Athens yields you up\n(Which by no means we may extenuate)\nTo death or to a vow of single life.\nCome, my Hippolyta. What cheer, my love?\nDemetrius and Egeus, go along.\nI must employ you in some business\nAgainst our nuptial and confer with you\nOf something nearly that concerns yourselves.\nEGEUS\nWith duty and desire we follow you.\nAll but Hermia and Lysander exit.\nLYSANDER\nHow now, my love? Why is your cheek so pale?\nHow chance the roses there do fade so fast?\nHERMIA\nBelike for want of rain, which I could well\nBeteem them from the tempest of my eyes.\nLYSANDER\nAy me! For aught that I could ever read,\nCould ever hear by tale or history,\nThe course of true love never did run smooth.\nBut either it was different in blood\nHERMIA\nO cross! Too high to be enthralled to low.\nLYSANDER\nOr else misgraffd in respect of years\nHERMIA\nO spite! Too old to be engaged to young.\nLYSANDER\nOr else it stood upon the choice of friends\nHERMIA\nO hell, to choose love by anothers eyes!\nLYSANDER\nOr, if there were a sympathy in choice,\nWar, death, or sickness did lay siege to it,\nMaking it momentany as a sound,\nSwift as a shadow, short as any dream,\nBrief as the lightning in the collied night,\nThat, in a spleen, unfolds both heaven and Earth,\nAnd, ere a man hath power to say Behold!\nThe jaws of darkness do devour it up.\nSo quick bright things come to confusion.\nHERMIA\nIf then true lovers have been ever crossed,\nIt stands as an edict in destiny.\nThen let us teach our trial patience\nBecause it is a customary cross,\nAs due to love as thoughts and dreams and sighs,\nWishes and tears, poor fancys followers.\nLYSANDER\nA good persuasion. Therefore, hear me, Hermia:\nI have a widow aunt, a dowager\nOf great revenue, and she hath no child.\nFrom Athens is her house remote seven leagues,\nAnd she respects me as her only son.\nThere, gentle Hermia, may I marry thee;\nAnd to that place the sharp Athenian law\nCannot pursue us. If thou lovest me, then\nSteal forth thy fathers house tomorrow night,\nAnd in the wood a league without the town\n(Where I did meet thee once with Helena\nTo do observance to a morn of May),\nThere will I stay for thee.\nHERMIA  My good Lysander,\nI swear to thee by Cupids strongest bow,\nBy his best arrow with the golden head,\nBy the simplicity of Venus doves,\nBy that which knitteth souls and prospers loves,\nAnd by that fire which burned the Carthage queen\nWhen the false Trojan under sail was seen,\nBy all the vows that ever men have broke\n(In number more than ever women spoke),\nIn that same place thou hast appointed me,\nTomorrow truly will I meet with thee.\nLYSANDER\nKeep promise, love. Look, here comes Helena.\n\nEnter Helena.\n\nHERMIA\nGodspeed, fair Helena. Whither away?\nHELENA\nCall you me fair? That fair again unsay.\nDemetrius loves your fair. O happy fair!\nYour eyes are lodestars and your tongues sweet air\nMore tunable than lark to shepherds ear\nWhen wheat is green, when hawthorn buds appear.\nSickness is catching. O, were favor so!\nYours would I catch, fair Hermia, ere I go.\nMy ear should catch your voice, my eye your eye;\nMy tongue should catch your tongues sweet\nmelody.\nWere the world mine, Demetrius being bated,\nThe rest Id give to be to you translated.\nO, teach me how you look and with what art\nYou sway the motion of Demetrius heart!\nHERMIA\nI frown upon him, yet he loves me still.\nHELENA\nO, that your frowns would teach my smiles such\nskill!\nHERMIA\nI give him curses, yet he gives me love.\nHELENA\nO, that my prayers could such affection move!\nHERMIA\nThe more I hate, the more he follows me.\nHELENA\nThe more I love, the more he hateth me.\nHERMIA\nHis folly, Helena, is no fault of mine.\nHELENA\nNone but your beauty. Would that fault were mine!\nHERMIA\nTake comfort: he no more shall see my face.\nLysander and myself will fly this place.\nBefore the time I did Lysander see\nSeemed Athens as a paradise to me.\nO, then, what graces in my love do dwell\nThat he hath turned a heaven unto a hell!\nLYSANDER\nHelen, to you our minds we will unfold.\nTomorrow night when Phoebe doth behold\nHer silver visage in the watry glass,\nDecking with liquid pearl the bladed grass\n(A time that lovers flights doth still conceal),\nThrough Athens gates have we devised to steal.\nHERMIA\nAnd in the wood where often you and I\nUpon faint primrose beds were wont to lie,\nEmptying our bosoms of their counsel sweet,\nThere my Lysander and myself shall meet\nAnd thence from Athens turn away our eyes\nTo seek new friends and stranger companies.\nFarewell, sweet playfellow. Pray thou for us,\nAnd good luck grant thee thy Demetrius.\nKeep word, Lysander. We must starve our sight\nFrom lovers food till morrow deep midnight.\nLYSANDER\nI will, my Hermia.\tHermia exits.\nHelena, adieu.\nAs you on him, Demetrius dote on you!\nLysander exits.\nHELENA\nHow happy some oer other some can be!\nThrough Athens I am thought as fair as she.\nBut what of that? Demetrius thinks not so.\nHe will not know what all but he do know.\nAnd, as he errs, doting on Hermias eyes,\nSo I, admiring of his qualities.\nThings base and vile, holding no quantity,\nLove can transpose to form and dignity.\nLove looks not with the eyes but with the mind;\nAnd therefore is winged Cupid painted blind.\nNor hath Loves mind of any judgment taste.\nWings, and no eyes, figure unheedy haste.\nAnd therefore is Love said to be a child\nBecause in choice he is so oft beguiled.\nAs waggish boys in game themselves forswear,\nSo the boy Love is perjured everywhere.\nFor, ere Demetrius looked on Hermias eyne,\nHe hailed down oaths that he was only mine;\nAnd when this hail some heat from Hermia felt,\nSo he dissolved, and showrs of oaths did melt.\nI will go tell him of fair Hermias flight.\nThen to the wood will he tomorrow night\nPursue her. And, for this intelligence\nIf I have thanks, it is a dear expense.\nBut herein mean I to enrich my pain,\nTo have his sight thither and back again.\nShe exits.\n\nScene 2\nEnter Quince the carpenter, and Snug the joiner, and\x0bBottom the weaver, and Flute the bellows-mender, and\x0bSnout the tinker, and Starveling the tailor.\n\nQUINCE  Is all our company here?\nBOTTOM  You were best to call them generally, man by\nman, according to the scrip.\nQUINCE  Here is the scroll of every mans name which\nis thought fit, through all Athens, to play in our\ninterlude before the Duke and the Duchess on his\nwedding day at night.\nBOTTOM  First, good Peter Quince, say what the play\ntreats on, then read the names of the actors, and so\ngrow to a point.\nQUINCE  Marry, our play is The most lamentable\ncomedy and most cruel death of Pyramus and\nThisbe.\nBOTTOM  A very good piece of work, I assure you, and a\nmerry. Now, good Peter Quince, call forth your\nactors by the scroll. Masters, spread yourselves.\nQUINCE  Answer as I call you. Nick Bottom, the weaver.\nBOTTOM  Ready. Name what part I am for, and\nproceed.\nQUINCE  You, Nick Bottom, are set down for Pyramus.\nBOTTOM  What is Pyramusa lover or a tyrant?\nQUINCE  A lover that kills himself most gallant for love.\nBOTTOM  That will ask some tears in the true performing\nof it. If I do it, let the audience look to their\neyes. I will move storms; I will condole in some\nmeasure. To the rest.Yet my chief humor is for a\ntyrant. I could play Ercles rarely, or a part to tear a\ncat in, to make all split:\n\n\tThe raging rocks\n\tAnd shivering shocks\n\tShall break the locks\n\t   Of prison gates.\n\tAnd Phibbus car\n\tShall shine from far\n\tAnd make and mar\n\t   The foolish Fates.\n\nThis was lofty. Now name the rest of the players.\nThis is Ercles vein, a tyrants vein. A lover is more\ncondoling.\nQUINCE  Francis Flute, the bellows-mender.\nFLUTE  Here, Peter Quince.\nQUINCE  Flute, you must take Thisbe on you.\nFLUTE  What is Thisbea wandring knight?\nQUINCE  It is the lady that Pyramus must love.\nFLUTE  Nay, faith, let not me play a woman. I have a\nbeard coming.\nQUINCE  Thats all one. You shall play it in a mask, and\nyou may speak as small as you will.\nBOTTOM  An I may hide my face, let me play Thisbe too.\nIll speak in a monstrous little voice: Thisne,\nThisne!Ah Pyramus, my lover dear! Thy Thisbe\ndear and lady dear!\nQUINCE  No, no, you must play Pyramusand, Flute,\nyou Thisbe.\nBOTTOM  Well, proceed.\nQUINCE  Robin Starveling, the tailor.\nSTARVELING  Here, Peter Quince.\nQUINCE  Robin Starveling, you must play Thisbes\nmother.Tom Snout, the tinker.\nSNOUT  Here, Peter Quince.\nQUINCE  You, Pyramus father.Myself, Thisbes\nfather.Snug the joiner, you the lions part.\nAnd I hope here is a play fitted.\nSNUG  Have you the lions part written? Pray you, if it\nbe, give it me, for I am slow of study.\nQUINCE  You may do it extempore, for it is nothing but\nroaring.\nBOTTOM  Let me play the lion too. I will roar that I will\ndo any mans heart good to hear me. I will roar that\nI will make the Duke say Let him roar again. Let\nhim roar again!\nQUINCE  An you should do it too terribly, you would\nfright the Duchess and the ladies that they would\nshriek, and that were enough to hang us all.\nALL  That would hang us, every mothers son.\nBOTTOM  I grant you, friends, if you should fright the\nladies out of their wits, they would have no more\ndiscretion but to hang us. But I will aggravate my\nvoice so that I will roar you as gently as any sucking\ndove. I will roar you an twere any nightingale.\nQUINCE  You can play no part but Pyramus, for Pyramus\nis a sweet-faced man, a proper man as one\nshall see in a summers day, a most lovely gentlemanlike\nman. Therefore you must needs play \nPyramus.\nBOTTOM  Well, I will undertake it. What beard were I\nbest to play it in?\nQUINCE  Why, what you will.\nBOTTOM  I will discharge it in either your straw-color\nbeard, your orange-tawny beard, your purple-in-grain\nbeard, or your French-crown-color beard,\nyour perfit yellow.\nQUINCE  Some of your French crowns have no hair at\nall, and then you will play barefaced. But, masters,\nhere are your parts, giving out the parts, and I am\nto entreat you, request you, and desire you to con\nthem by tomorrow night and meet me in the palace\nwood, a mile without the town, by moonlight. There\nwill we rehearse, for if we meet in the city, we shall\nbe dogged with company and our devices known. In\nthe meantime I will draw a bill of properties such as\nour play wants. I pray you fail me not.\nBOTTOM  We will meet, and there we may rehearse\nmost obscenely and courageously. Take pains. Be\nperfit. Adieu.\nQUINCE  At the Dukes Oak we meet.\nBOTTOM  Enough. Hold or cut bowstrings.\n'

Tokenize the words and remove stopwords

['enter theseus, hippolyta, and philostrate, with others.',
 'theseus\nnow, fair hippolyta, our nuptial hour\ndraws on apace.',
 'four happy days bring in\nanother moon.',
 'but, o, methinks how slow\nthis old moon wanes!',
 'she lingers my desires\nlike to a stepdame or a dowager\nlong withering out a young mans revenue.',
 'hippolyta\nfour days will quickly steep themselves in night;\nfour nights will quickly dream away the time;\nand then the moon, like to a silver bow\nnew-bent in heaven, shall behold the night\nof our solemnities.',
 'theseus  go, philostrate,\nstir up the athenian youth to merriments.',
 'awake the pert and nimble spirit of mirth.',
 'turn melancholy forth to funerals;\nthe pale companion is not for our pomp.',
 'philostrate exits.',
 'hippolyta, i wooed thee with my sword\nand won thy love doing thee injuries,\nbut i will wed thee in another key,\nwith pomp, with triumph, and with reveling.',
 'enter egeus and his daughter hermia, and lysander \x0b and demetrius.',
 'egeus\nhappy be theseus, our renownd duke!',
 'theseus\nthanks, good egeus.',
 'whats the news with thee?',
 'egeus\nfull of vexation come i, with complaint\nagainst my child, my daughter hermia.',
 'stand forth, demetrius.my noble lord,\nthis man hath my consent to marry her.',
 'stand forth, lysander.and, my gracious duke,\nthis man hath bewitched the bosom of my child.',
 'thou, thou, lysander, thou hast given her rhymes\nand interchanged love tokens with my child.',
 'thou hast by moonlight at her window sung\nwith feigning voice verses of feigning love\nand stoln the impression of her fantasy\nwith bracelets of thy hair, rings, gauds, conceits,\nknacks, trifles, nosegays, sweetmeatsmessengers\nof strong prevailment in unhardened youth.',
 'with cunning hast thou filched my daughters heart,\nturned her obedience (which is due to me)\nto stubborn harshness.and, my gracious duke,\nbe it so she will not here before your grace\nconsent to marry with demetrius,\ni beg the ancient privilege of athens:\nas she is mine, i may dispose of her,\nwhich shall be either to this gentleman\nor to her death, according to our law\nimmediately provided in that case.',
 'theseus\nwhat say you, hermia?',
 'be advised, fair maid.',
 'to you, your father should be as a god,\none that composed your beauties, yea, and one\nto whom you are but as a form in wax\nby him imprinted, and within his power\nto leave the figure or disfigure it.',
 'demetrius is a worthy gentleman.',
 'hermia\nso is lysander.',
 'theseus  in himself he is,\nbut in this kind, wanting your fathers voice,\nthe other must be held the worthier.',
 'hermia\ni would my father looked but with my eyes.',
 'theseus\nrather your eyes must with his judgment look.',
 'hermia\ni do entreat your grace to pardon me.',
 'i know not by what power i am made bold,\nnor how it may concern my modesty\nin such a presence here to plead my thoughts;\nbut i beseech your grace that i may know\nthe worst that may befall me in this case\nif i refuse to wed demetrius.',
 'theseus\neither to die the death or to abjure\nforever the society of men.',
 'therefore, fair hermia, question your desires,\nknow of your youth, examine well your blood,\nwhether (if you yield not to your fathers choice)\nyou can endure the livery of a nun,\nfor aye to be in shady cloister mewed,\nto live a barren sister all your life,\nchanting faint hymns to the cold fruitless moon.',
 'thrice-blessd they that master so their blood\nto undergo such maiden pilgrimage,\nbut earthlier happy is the rose distilled\nthan that which, withering on the virgin thorn,\ngrows, lives, and dies in single blessedness.',
 'hermia\nso will i grow, so live, so die, my lord,\nere i will yield my virgin patent up\nunto his lordship whose unwishd yoke\nmy soul consents not to give sovereignty.',
 'theseus\ntake time to pause, and by the next new moon\n(the sealing day betwixt my love and me\nfor everlasting bond of fellowship),\nupon that day either prepare to die\nfor disobedience to your fathers will,\nor else to wed demetrius, as he would,\nor on dianas altar to protest\nfor aye austerity and single life.',
 'demetrius\nrelent, sweet hermia, and, lysander, yield\nthy crazd title to my certain right.',
 'lysander\nyou have her fathers love, demetrius.',
 'let me have hermias.',
 'do you marry him.',
 'egeus\nscornful lysander, true, he hath my love;\nand what is mine my love shall render him.',
 'and she is mine, and all my right of her\ni do estate unto demetrius.',
 'lysander, to theseus\ni am, my lord, as well derived as he,\nas well possessed.',
 'my love is more than his;\nmy fortunes every way as fairly ranked\n(if not with vantage) as demetrius;\nand (which is more than all these boasts can be)\ni am beloved of beauteous hermia.',
 'why should not i then prosecute my right?',
 'demetrius, ill avouch it to his head,\nmade love to nedars daughter, helena,\nand won her soul; and she, sweet lady, dotes,\ndevoutly dotes, dotes in idolatry,\nupon this spotted and inconstant man.',
 'theseus\ni must confess that i have heard so much,\nand with demetrius thought to have spoke thereof;\nbut, being overfull of self-affairs,\nmy mind did lose it.but, demetrius, come,\nand come, egeus; you shall go with me.',
 'i have some private schooling for you both.',
 'for you, fair hermia, look you arm yourself\nto fit your fancies to your fathers will,\nor else the law of athens yields you up\n(which by no means we may extenuate)\nto death or to a vow of single life.',
 'come, my hippolyta.',
 'what cheer, my love?',
 'demetrius and egeus, go along.',
 'i must employ you in some business\nagainst our nuptial and confer with you\nof something nearly that concerns yourselves.',
 'egeus\nwith duty and desire we follow you.',
 'all but hermia and lysander exit.',
 'lysander\nhow now, my love?',
 'why is your cheek so pale?',
 'how chance the roses there do fade so fast?',
 'hermia\nbelike for want of rain, which i could well\nbeteem them from the tempest of my eyes.',
 'lysander\nay me!',
 'for aught that i could ever read,\ncould ever hear by tale or history,\nthe course of true love never did run smooth.',
 'but either it was different in blood\nhermia\no cross!',
 'too high to be enthralled to low.',
 'lysander\nor else misgraffd in respect of years\nhermia\no spite!',
 'too old to be engaged to young.',
 'lysander\nor else it stood upon the choice of friends\nhermia\no hell, to choose love by anothers eyes!',
 'lysander\nor, if there were a sympathy in choice,\nwar, death, or sickness did lay siege to it,\nmaking it momentany as a sound,\nswift as a shadow, short as any dream,\nbrief as the lightning in the collied night,\nthat, in a spleen, unfolds both heaven and earth,\nand, ere a man hath power to say behold!',
 'the jaws of darkness do devour it up.',
 'so quick bright things come to confusion.',
 'hermia\nif then true lovers have been ever crossed,\nit stands as an edict in destiny.',
 'then let us teach our trial patience\nbecause it is a customary cross,\nas due to love as thoughts and dreams and sighs,\nwishes and tears, poor fancys followers.',
 'lysander\na good persuasion.',
 'therefore, hear me, hermia:\ni have a widow aunt, a dowager\nof great revenue, and she hath no child.',
 'from athens is her house remote seven leagues,\nand she respects me as her only son.',

 'hermia  my good lysander,\ni swear to thee by cupids strongest bow,\nby his best arrow with the golden head,\nby the simplicity of venus doves,\nby that which knitteth souls and prospers loves,\nand by that fire which burned the carthage queen\nwhen the false trojan under sail was seen,\nby all the vows that ever men have broke\n(in number more than ever women spoke),\nin that same place thou hast appointed me,\ntomorrow truly will i meet with thee.',
 'lysander\nkeep promise, love.',
 'look, here comes helena.',
 'enter helena.',
 'hermia\ngodspeed, fair helena.',
 'whither away?',
 'helena\ncall you me fair?',
 'that fair again unsay.',
 'demetrius loves your fair.',
 'o happy fair!',
 'your eyes are lodestars and your tongues sweet air\nmore tunable than lark to shepherds ear\nwhen wheat is green, when hawthorn buds appear.',
 'sickness is catching.',
 'o, were favor so!',
 'yours would i catch, fair hermia, ere i go.',
 'my ear should catch your voice, my eye your eye;\nmy tongue should catch your tongues sweet\nmelody.',
 'were the world mine, demetrius being bated,\nthe rest id give to be to you translated.',
 'o, teach me how you look and with what art\nyou sway the motion of demetrius heart!',
 'hermia\ni frown upon him, yet he loves me still.',
 'helena\no, that your frowns would teach my smiles such\nskill!',
 'hermia\ni give him curses, yet he gives me love.',
 'helena\no, that my prayers could such affection move!',
 'hermia\nthe more i hate, the more he follows me.',
 'helena\nthe more i love, the more he hateth me.',
 'hermia\nhis folly, helena, is no fault of mine.',
 'helena\nnone but your beauty.',
 'would that fault were mine!',
 'hermia\ntake comfort: he no more shall see my face.',
 'lysander and myself will fly this place.',
 'before the time i did lysander see\nseemed athens as a paradise to me.',
 'o, then, what graces in my love do dwell\nthat he hath turned a heaven unto a hell!',
 'lysander\nhelen, to you our minds we will unfold.',
 'tomorrow night when phoebe doth behold\nher silver visage in the watry glass,\ndecking with liquid pearl the bladed grass\n(a time that lovers flights doth still conceal),\nthrough athens gates have we devised to steal.',

from nltk.corpus import wordnet
def bendict(word):
    for ss in wordnet.synsets(word):
        print(ss, ss.definition())

bendict('hermia')

bendict('quince')

Synset('quince.n.01') small Asian tree with pinkish flowers and pear-shaped fruit; widely cultivated
Synset('quince.n.02') aromatic acid-tasting pear-shaped fruit used in preserves
